diagnosed metastatic ER+ breast cancer" or "cycle 3 day 1 of irinotecan for metastatic TNBC").

For Patient type — determining "New patient" vs "Follow up":
- "New patient" if ANY of these apply:
  * Note says "New Patient Evaluation", "Consult Note", "consultation", "establish care", "referred by", or "second opinion"
  * This is the patient's FIRST visit to this oncologist/clinic (even if they had surgery or treatment elsewhere)
  * Post-surgery first visit to medical oncology to discuss adjuvant/systemic therapy
- "Follow up" ONLY if the patient has been seen by THIS provider/clinic before for ongoing management
- When in doubt, "New patient" is safer than "Follow up"

For second opinion — write "yes" ONLY if the note explicitly mentions "second opinion", "another opinion", or the patient was clearly referred for an independent review of their case. Do NOT guess.

', 'Cancer_Diagnosis': 'TASK: Extract cancer diagnosis details.

For Type_of_Cancer, be SPECIFIC — include as much of the following as the note provides:
1. Histologic subtype: IDC (invasive ductal carcinoma), ILC (invasive lobular carcinoma), DCIS, mixed, etc.
2. Receptor status — ER, PR, and HER2. e.g., "ER+/PR+/HER2-"
3. If receptor status changed between original diagnosis and metastatic/recurrent biopsy, note BOTH.
- Example: "ER+/PR+/HER2- invasive ductal carcinoma" NOT just "breast cancer"
- Example with change: "Originally ER+/PR+/HER2-, metastatic biopsy ER+/PR-/HER2-"
Do NOT write just "breast cancer" or "MBC" — always include the subtype and receptor status when available in the note.
IMPORTANT: Search the ENTIRE note for receptor status — it may appear in PMH, Problem List, Pathology, HPI, or Diagnosis section, not just the Assessment. If ER/PR/HER2 status is stated ANYWHERE, include it. Do NOT omit HER2 status just because it appears only in the Past Medical History.

HER2 status rules — follow strictly:
- If the note explicitly states HER2 result (positive, negative, equivocal, IHC score, FISH result), write it.
- If HER2 was NOT tested or NOT mentioned in the note, write "HER2: not tested" — do NOT guess or assume HER2- or HER2+.
- DCIS (ductal carcinoma in situ) often does NOT have HER2 testing. Do NOT assume HER2- for DCIS unless explicitly stated.
- HER2 IHC scoring — use this table to determine HER2 status:
  IHC 0     = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 1+    = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 2+    = EQUIVOCAL — must check FISH/ISH result:
    IHC 2+ with FISH ratio ≥ 2.0 (or gene copies ≥ 6) = HER2 POSITIVE
    IHC 2+ with FISH ratio < 2.0 (or "FISH negative")  = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 3+    = HER2 POSITIVE
- FISH interpretation (when reported without IHC):
  FISH ratio ≥ 2.0 = amplified = HER2 POSITIVE
  FISH ratio < 2.0 = not amplified = HER2 NEGATIVE
- IMPORTANT: If treatment includes Herceptin/trastuzumab/pertuzumab/T-DM1/T-DXd/Tykerb/lapatinib/TCHP/THP/ado-trastuzumab, the cancer is HER2+. Do NOT write HER2-.
- IMPORTANT: If the note says "triple negative" ANYWHERE (HPI, A/P, diagnosis), that means ER-/PR-/HER2-. Write HER2 as NEGATIVE even if the HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] or masked. "Triple negative" is a definitive classification.
- IMPORTANT: If HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] but the Assessment/Plan writes "[REDACTED] negative" in the receptor status line, infer HER2 = negative.
- IMPORTANT: goserelin (Zoladex) / leuprolide (Lupron) used DURING CHEMOTHERAPY for fertility preservation does NOT indicate ER+. These drugs suppress ovaries to protect eggs during chemo. Only infer ER+ from goserelin/Lupron when it is used as ENDOCRINE THERAPY (adjuvant or palliative, continuous long-term use for hormone suppression). If the note says "goserelin for fertility preservation" or "goserelin during chemotherapy", do NOT add "ER+ (inferred from goserelin)".
- IMPORTANT: If the patient has MULTIPLE primary cancers (e.g., gastric cancer AND breast cancer), report receptor status ONLY for the BREAST cancer. Do NOT mix receptor status from other cancers. For example, if gastric cancer is HER2+ but breast cancer is HER2-, write HER2- for the breast cancer.

For Stage_of_Cancer:
- Write the AJCC/TNM stage if stated (e.g., "Stage IIA", "pT2N0M0", "Stage IV")
- If the patient was originally diagnosed at one stage but now has metastatic recurrence, write BOTH:
  e.g., "Originally Stage [X], now metastatic (Stage IV)"
  IMPORTANT: Only write "Originally Stage [X]" if the note EXPLICITLY states the original stage number (e.g., "Stage IIA at diagnosis"). If the note only says "metastatic recurrence" without specifying the original stage, write just "Metastatic (Stage IV)" — do NOT guess or invent the original stage.
- If distant metastases are documented, the current stage is Stage IV even if not explicitly stated.
- If the Assessment/Plan states a stage (e.g., "Stage I", "early stage"), extract it — do NOT write "Not mentioned in note".
- If tumor size and node status are available, infer the approximate stage (e.g., "2.3 cm, 0/3 LN negative — approximately Stage I-II").
- IMPORTANT: Node-negative ("node neg", "0/N LN+") with small tumor (≤2cm) = Stage I, NOT Stage II.
  Stage II requires either positive lymph nodes OR tumor >2cm. "Early stage node negative" = Stage I.
- Only write "Not mentioned in note" if there is truly NO staging, tumor size, or node information anywhere in the note.

Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
"Type_of_Cancer": "Specific cancer type with histologic subtype and receptor statuses when available. e.g., 'ER+/PR+/HER2- invasive ductal carcinoma'",
"Stage_of_Cancer": "AJCC stage, or original + current if recurred/progressed. e.g., 'Stage IIA' or 'Originally Stage [X], now metastatic (Stage IV)' — only include original stage if explicitly stated in the note",
"Distant Metastasis": "if there is distant metastasis (met). Yes, to where; No; or Not sure. Note: axillary lymph nodes are REGIONAL, not distant.",
"Metastasis": "if there is met, Yes (to where), No, or Not sure"
}

', 'Treatment_Goals': 'TASK: Extract the goal/intent of cancer treatment.

Write the INTENT of the cancer treatment itself: "curative", "palliative", "risk reduction", "adjuvant", or "symptom management".
Do NOT write the purpose of this visit (e.g., "follow-up", "discuss treatment options", "review treatment plan").

FIRST — check the note for EXPLICIT intent words:
- If the note says "palliative" or "palliative intent" or "palliative care" → write "palliative". Done.
- If the note says "curative" or "curative intent" → write "curative". Done.
- If the note says "adjuvant" → this describes the treatment TYPE, not the GOAL. For non-metastatic cancer, adjuvant treatment has curative intent → write "curative". Only write "palliative" if metastatic.
- If the note explicitly states an intent, USE IT. Do NOT override the physician's own words.

ONLY if no explicit intent word is found, use this decision tree:
- Early-stage cancer + surgery + adjuvant chemo/radiation/endocrine therapy → "curative" or "adjuvant"
- Neoadjuvant chemotherapy before surgery for curable cancer → "curative" (NOT "neoadjuvant")
- Metastatic/Stage IV cancer → "palliative"
- DCIS only, no invasive cancer → "risk reduction"
- Completed curative treatment, now on surveillance endocrine therapy → "adjuvant" or "risk reduction"
- Do NOT write "risk reduction" for early-stage invasive cancer receiving adjuvant chemo — that is "curative" or "adjuvant".
- Do NOT write "neoadjuvant" as a goal — write "curative" instead.

Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema (NO other keys):
{
"goals_of_treatment": "curative, palliative, adjuvant, risk reduction, or symptom management"
}
', 'Response_Assessment': 'TASK: Extract how the cancer is CURRENTLY responding to treatment.

Think step by step before answering:
1. Has this patient started any cancer treatment yet? (surgery alone does not count as "treatment" for response assessment purposes)
   CRITICAL EXCEPTION — POST-NEOADJUVANT SURGERY: If the patient received neoadjuvant therapy (chemo, targeted therapy, etc.) BEFORE surgery, the surgical pathology IS the response assessment. Describe the pathologic response:
   - Residual tumor size and cellularity (e.g., "3.84 cm residual tumor, 5% cellularity")
   - Lymph node status post-treatment (e.g., "3/28 LN positive with treatment effect")
   - Whether pathologic complete response (pCR) was achieved or not
   Do NOT write "Not yet on treatment" for a patient who completed neoadjuvant therapy and had surgery.
   This includes patients who had PROGRESSION on neoadjuvant therapy and then went to surgery — the progression itself IS the response (e.g., "disease progressed on neoadjuvant AC/T, proceeded to bilateral mastectomies"). Describe BOTH the treatment response AND the surgical pathology.
   IMPORTANT: Completed radiation therapy COUNTS as treatment. If the patient recently finished radiation, they HAVE received treatment — do NOT write "Not yet on treatment". "Not yet on treatment" is ONLY correct if no cancer treatment (chemo, radiation, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy) has been given at all.
   IMPORTANT: A patient who was on PREVIOUS treatment and now shows disease PROGRESSION or RECURRENCE — that IS a response assessment (the response is "progression"). Even if they are switching to a new regimen or between treatments, document the current disease status from imaging/labs.
   For LOCAL RECURRENCE: describe the recurrence factually (e.g., "local recurrence in left breast, 1.7cm IDC on excisional biopsy"). Do NOT write "cancer is not responding to treatment" — the patient may have discontinued treatment or completed it years ago. Focus on WHAT happened (recurrence), not WHY.
   IMPORTANT: For patients on adjuvant/maintenance therapy, "no evidence of recurrence" or "no evidence of disease" on exam or imaging IS a valid response assessment — it means the treatment is working.
   IMPORTANT — the patient IS on treatment if ANY of these clues appear:
   - "currently on [drug]", "taking [drug]", "continue [drug]", "on [drug] since [date]"
   - Mentions of chemotherapy cycles, off weeks, dose adjustments (e.g., "off week", "cycle 3", "due to start next cycle")
   - "labs okay to proceed with chemotherapy" or similar pre-cycle clearance
   - Current medications include any oncologic drug (chemo, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy)
   - Active medication list contains chemotherapy drugs (Xeloda, ixabepilone, paclitaxel, etc.)
   Do NOT be confused by "off week" — a patient on an off week between chemo cycles IS on treatment.
2. If yes, what treatment are they on? (chemo, endocrine therapy, targeted therapy, immunotherapy)
3. Is there any evidence of how the cancer is responding? Search the ENTIRE note for ANY of these:
   - Imaging: CT stable, PET response, scan results
   - Tumor markers: CA 15-3, CEA rising or falling
   - Physical exam: mass shrinking, "breaking up", palpable changes, skin changes improving
   - Clinical status: "labs okay to proceed" (implies stable enough to continue)
   - Symptom changes: "able to do more", "pain improving", "up and about"
   Even brief exam comments like "area appears to be breaking up" count as response evidence.
4. If no treatment started yet, write "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess."
5. If on treatment but no response data in the note, write "Not mentioned in note."
   BEFORE writing "Not mentioned", verify there is truly NO evidence anywhere in the note. These ALL count as response evidence:
   - "stopped [drug] for progression" or "disease progressed on [drug]" → response is PROGRESSION
   - New metastatic sites (brain, lung, liver, bone mets) → response is PROGRESSION
   - "exam stable" or "no new findings" → response is STABLE DISEASE
   - Tumor marker trends (rising or falling)
   - Post-surgical pathology after neoadjuvant therapy
   If ANY of the above is present, do NOT write "Not mentioned" — describe the evidence.
6. TEMPORAL ATTRIBUTION — critical rule:
   When citing imaging (PET, CT, MRI, bone scan), verify the scan was done AFTER the current treatment regimen started.
   If the patient switched treatments (e.g., from letrozole to Faslodex), a scan done BEFORE the switch reflects the PRIOR treatment's response, not the current one.
   Example: PET on 10/10 showed progression → patient switched to Faslodex on 10/16 → current visit exam says "stable" → the response on CURRENT treatment is "exam stable", NOT "PET showed progression on Faslodex."

STRICT RULES — do NOT violate:
- Do NOT include future plans ("will start", "plan to", "she will have radiation").
- Do NOT include treatment discussions or recommendations.
- Do NOT use generic boilerplate phrases. Every claim must be traceable to a specific sentence in the note.
- Do NOT write "tolerating therapy well" or "symptoms improved" unless the note EXPLICITLY uses those exact words.
- Do NOT use the word "responding" or "response" unless you can cite SPECIFIC imaging or tumor marker evidence. "Labs okay" or "stable on exam" alone does NOT mean the cancer is "responding" — it means the cancer status is unknown or stable.
- IMPORTANT: "responding" implies MEASURABLE IMPROVEMENT. Use "stable disease" for unchanged findings, "no evidence of disease" for no measurable disease, and "responding" ONLY when there is documented tumor shrinkage or marker decrease.
- Do NOT describe pathology results (tumor size, grade, margins) as a "response" — pathology is a diagnosis, not a treatment response.
- Do NOT describe surgical recovery ("healing well", "recovering from surgery") as a response assessment.
- Do NOT describe risk estimates or Oncotype scores as a response — those are prognostic tools, not treatment responses.
- Do NOT write "Not applicable" or "Not specified".
Examples:
- GOOD: "CT stable, CA 15-3 decreased from 28 to 14"
- GOOD: "no evidence of disease recurrence on imaging and exam"
- GOOD: "PET-CT shows stable to slightly decreased metabolic activity of osseous metastases"
- GOOD: "Axillary LN decreased from 8cm to 2-3cm after 2 cycles AC"
- GOOD: "Disease progressed on neoadjuvant AC/T, proceeded to bilateral mastectomies. Surgical pathology showed residual 2.5cm tumor with 5/12 positive lymph nodes."
- GOOD: "Stopped neoadjuvant therapy due to progression. S/p mastectomy with residual disease."
- BAD: "tolerating therapy well, symptoms improved" (generic boilerplate)
- BAD: "She will continue on letrozole and have radiation" (this is a plan, not a response)
- BAD: "0.9 cm grade 1 IDC, margins negative" (this is pathology, not treatment response)
- BAD: "Healing well from surgery" (surgical recovery, not cancer treatment response)
- BAD: "Low Risk Oncotype score" (prognostic tool, not a response to treatment)
- BAD: "Not mentioned in note" when the note says "stopped chemo for progression" or describes new brain/lung/bone metastases — these ARE response evidence (progression)
- BAD: "Not yet on treatment" for a patient who completed neoadjuvant chemo + surgery — the surgical pathology IS the response
- BAD: "PET showed progression on Faslodex" when the PET was done BEFORE Faslodex was started — wrong temporal attribution

Write your reasoning first, then output the JSON on a new line starting with {
IMPORTANT: Your JSON must contain ONLY the key "response_assessment". Do NOT add any other keys.

Respond with a JSON object using this exact schema (NO other keys):
{
"response_assessment": "How is the cancer CURRENTLY responding? Cite specific evidence: imaging, labs, exam. Do NOT include future plans, surgical recovery, or prognostic scores."
}
'}

', 'Procedure_Plan': 'TASK: Extract the 'Procedure Plan' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.
Include ONLY future/planned procedures: surgery, biopsy, lumbar puncture, Chemo Port Insertion, or other interventional procedures.

IMPORTANT — look for PRE-TREATMENT PREREQUISITES. When chemotherapy is planned, there are often preparatory steps mentioned with phrases like:
- "prior to starting", "before initiating", "in order to start", "steps required"
- "needs to undergo", "baseline", "will need"
Common pre-chemo procedures: port placement (Mediport), chemotherapy teaching session, dental clearance.
These ARE procedures and MUST be included.

CRITICAL — distinguish PAST from FUTURE:
- PAST (DO NOT include): "underwent mastectomy on 10/29/16", "s/p colectomy in June 2018", "had biopsy on 05/25", "completed radiation"
- FUTURE (include): "will schedule biopsy", "plan for port placement", "surgery pending", "scheduled for colectomy next week", "needs port placement", "steps including port placement"
If a procedure has a past date or uses past tense (underwent, had, completed, s/p), it is PAST — do NOT include it.

Also DO NOT include:
- Imaging plans (CT, MRI, PET, PET/CT, DEXA, bone scan, ultrasound, mammogram, echocardiogram/TTE, X-ray — these ALL go in Imaging_Plan, NOT here)
- Radiation/radiotherapy plans (radiation, XRT, RT, chest wall RT, whole brain radiation — these go in radiotherapy_plan, NOT here)
- Lab plans or blood tests
- Medication/treatment plans (e.g., "continue arimidex", "could use faslodex" — these go in Medication_Plan)
- Referrals or consultations (e.g., "XRT consult" is a referral, not a procedure)
- General treatment goals or contingency plans
- Genetic/molecular testing (Foundation One, Oncotype, BRCA panels — these go in Genetic_Testing_Plan)
- Fertility preservation referrals (these go in Referral → Others)

Common mistakes to AVOID:
- "Brain MRI" → this is IMAGING, not a procedure
- "chest wall RT" → this is RADIOTHERAPY, not a procedure
- "will proceed with radiation" → this is RADIOTHERAPY, not a procedure
- "TTE" or "echocardiogram" → this is IMAGING, not a procedure
- "DEXA scan" → this is IMAGING, not a procedure
- "chemotherapy", "TCHP", "FOLFOX", "AC-T" → these are MEDICATIONS/SYSTEMIC THERAPY, not procedures
- "start letrozole", "continue tamoxifen" → these are MEDICATION plans, not procedures
- "immunotherapy", "targeted therapy" → these are MEDICATIONS, not procedures
If no future procedures are planned, say "No procedures planned."
Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
    "procedure_plan": "A summary of any planned procedures, or 'No procedures planned.'"
}
', 'Imaging_Plan': 'TASK: Extract the 'Imaging Plan' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.
Include ONLY future/planned imaging: CT, MRI, PET/CT, ultrasound, DEXA scans, bone scan, X-ray, echocardiogram (TTE/Echo), mammogram.
Include timing and rationale if mentioned (e.g., "CT in 3 months for restaging").

IMPORTANT — look for PRE-TREATMENT IMAGING. When chemotherapy (especially anthracyclines like AC, doxorubicin) is planned, an echocardiogram (TTE/Echo) is often required as a baseline cardiac assessment. Look for phrases like:
- "prior to starting", "before initiating", "in order to start", "steps required", "baseline"
- "will need TTE", "order echocardiogram", "Echo q6 months"
Echocardiogram/TTE IS an imaging study and MUST be included here.

CRITICAL — distinguish PAST from FUTURE:
- PAST (DO NOT include): "MRI showed lesions", "CT on 12/24/2019 showed...", "bone density scan last July showed...", "Echo was normal" (past result)
- FUTURE (include): "will order MRI", "DEXA in 1 year, due July 2019", "scans again in 3 months", "annual mammogram next due July 2019", "consider brain MRI if worsening", "needs TTE before starting chemo"

DO NOT include any procedure plan, lab plan, or medication plan.
List SPECIFIC imaging types. Do NOT use vague terms like "staging workup".
If no future imaging is planned, say "No imaging planned."

', 'Lab_Plan': 'TASK: Extract the 'lab Plan' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.

Also include pre-chemo blood draws (e.g., "blood draw prior to cycle", "labs before chemo", "draw and visit").

- Imaging scans: PET CT, CT, MRI, DEXA, bone scan, ultrasound, mammogram → these go in Imaging_Plan
- Genomic/molecular tests: MammaPrint, Oncotype DX, BRCA panel, NGS, liquid biopsy → these go in Genetic_Testing_Plan
- Treatment plans: medication changes, chemotherapy plans → these go in Medication_Plan
Lab_Plan is ONLY for blood draws and lab work.

', 'Genetic_Testing_Plan': 'TASK: Extract the 'Genetic Testing Plan' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.

1. Scope of section:
- First, find the 'Assessment/Plan' section of the note (usually the final section).
- Only look for plans that are clearly about FUTURE genetic or molecular TESTING.

2. What COUNTS as 'genetic or molecular testing':
- Diagnostic, prognostic, or predictive laboratory assays, such as:
  - Tumor genomic sequencing / NGS / panel testing
  - Germline genetic panels (e.g., hereditary cancer panel, BRCA testing)
  - Liquid biopsy / circulating tumor DNA (ctDNA) tests
  - Specific biomarker tests (e.g., PD-L1 testing, MSI testing, EGFR mutation testing)
  - Any plan to "send", "order", "check", or "obtain" a genetic, genomic, or molecular TEST

3. What MUST be EXCLUDED:
- DO NOT include any medications, systemic therapies, or treatment plans:
  - Chemotherapy, immunotherapy, targeted therapies (e.g., FGFR inhibitor, PARP inhibitor, CDK4/6 inhibitor, TKIs)
  - Hormonal therapy, radiation therapy, surgery
  - Clinical trial options, even if the trial involves targeted drugs or inhibitors
- DO NOT include tests that are already completed, historic, or only mentioned in past oncology history.
- DO NOT include imaging (CT, PET, MRI, X-ray, ultrasound) or routine labs (CBC, CMP).
- DO NOT include IHC staining (ER/PR/HER2 status confirmation), FISH testing, or biopsy for receptor status — these are standard pathology, NOT genetic testing.
- DO NOT include LVEF/echocardiogram, tumor markers (CA 15-3, CEA, CA 27.29), or other clinical lab rechecks.
- DO NOT include vague "recheck" or "would recheck" statements that refer to lab values, imaging, or cardiac function — these are NOT genetic testing.
- Oncotype DX, MammaPrint, Foundation One, Guardant360, BRCA panels, germline testing — these ARE genetic/molecular testing.

4. CRITICAL — do NOT include content that belongs in OTHER fields:
- Treatment contingency plans (e.g., "if progression, could use faslodex") → these go in Medication_Plan/Therapy_plan
- Imaging plans (PET CT, MRI) → these go in Imaging_Plan
- Response assessment text (e.g., "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess") → this is NOT genetic testing

5. If there is NO new genetic or molecular test planned in the Assessment/Plan:
- Set the value to: "None planned."

6. Output format:

{
    "genetic_testing_plan": "A summary of any future planned genetic or molecular tests, or a clear statement that none are planned."

- INCOMING (do NOT include): The patient was SENT TO this doctor by someone else. E.g., "referred by Dr. Smith", "referred to us for evaluation", "referred for consideration of adjunct therapy".

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a follow-up visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYour chest area is more tender, red, and swollen, which could be a sign of an infection. Your back pain has gotten worse, possibly because the cancer has grown. You have low levels of red blood cells, which is due to the cancer and the chemotherapy. You have low levels of sodium and potassium in your blood. Your treatment has been adjusted because it was causing side effects.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYour irinotecan treatment has been changed to every other week, and the dose has been increased. You have been given a new antibiotic, Doxycycline, to treat the possible infection. Your pain and nausea medications have been adjusted. You are also taking potassium supplements to help with low potassium levels.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to a radiation oncologist for further treatment. You will have more scans in 3 months to check on your condition. You will have blood tests every 4 months to check for signs of hepatitis B. You will have a follow-up visit in 2 weeks. You are referred to social work and home health services for additional support.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a medical oncology consult regarding your newly diagnosed breast cancer.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which means the cancer started in the milk ducts. It is in the upper-outer part of your right breast and is about 1.7 cm in size. The cancer is positive for estrogen receptors and negative for HER2. A lymph node in your armpit is also affected. No other specific imaging findings or physical exam findings are noted in this visit.\nA PET scan and genetic testing are pending.\nThe goal of your treatment is to cure the cancer.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nWe discussed the role of chemotherapy to help reduce the risk of the cancer coming back in other parts of your body. We also talked about the need for surgery and radiation to lower the risk of the cancer coming back in the breast area.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nA PET scan is pending, and we will follow up after the results are back.\nGenetic testing is also pending.\nYou are a full code, which means you want all possible life-saving measures if needed.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

She doesn't take anything for pain. She continues to use the Chinese herb pain patch as needed which helps. She feels the pain is related to her arthritis.     The hot flashes are less now.     Eating good. She doesn't sleep much at night which she feels is related to aging.     She continues to do exercises - stretching at home, sometimes does walking.     No shortness of breath. She has occasional cough which she feels is allergy related.     The muscle cramps continue, mostly at night, better when she stretches/exercise more.     No stomach pain. Sometimes certain foods can cause stomach discomfort. No nausea and vomiting. Stools are loose. Whenever she eats, she has a bowel movements. Sometimes have loose BMs 2-3x a day. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a follow-up visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nThe patient is without any evidence of disease recurrence on imaging, exam, and review of systems.\nHer leg, upper back, and arm pain have improved after completing physical therapy.\nThe patient's bone density in the low back has slightly improved, but it is still in the osteopenia range.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will continue to take Letrozole to help prevent the cancer from coming back.\nYou will also continue to take calcium and Vitamin D supplements to help with your bone health.\nYou can consider taking an over-the-counter magnesium supplement for occasional muscle cramping.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nIf your bone density falls below a certain level, you will need to see a dentist and then we could start you on a medication called Prolia.\nYou will have an annual mammogram of your right breast next due in July 2019.\nYou will have a bone density scan in 1 year to check for any changes, due in July 2019.\nYou are referred to have a brain MRI if your headaches worsen.\nYou are scheduled for a follow-up visit in 6 months or sooner if you have new or worsening symptoms.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

The immunohistochemical staining pattern supports the morphologic  findings. Positive staining for ***** along with positive MPO in  histiocytes is compatible with ***** ***** lymphadenitis. Positive  staining for T cell markers without loss of specific markers, favors a  non-neoplastic process. The CD30 stain highlights ***** and  negative staining for CD15 rules against Hodgkin lymphoma. B cell  markers are negative, ruling against a B cell lymphoma. Negative  staining for keratin and CD68 and ***** rules against a histiocytic  process or fulminant treatment effect in the lymph node. Overall, the  findings support a diagnosis of necrotizing lymphadenitis. The  differential diagnosis includes ***** ***** disease, which is  favored, as well as lymphadenitis secondary to systemic lupus  erythematous. "Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a consultation to discuss treatment options and to establish care for your breast cancer.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which started in the milk ducts. It is HER2 positive and estrogen receptor negative. The cancer was originally at stage II-III.\nThe cancer has not spread to other parts of your body.\nYou had surgery to remove the tumor and check the lymph nodes under your arm. The surgery showed no cancer left in the breast tissue, but some cancer was found in the lymph nodes.\nYou have completed the treatment before surgery and had a lumpectomy (surgery to remove the tumor) and surgery to remove and check the lymph nodes under your arm. The current assessment is based on the results of the surgery and imaging tests.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou are now being recommended to start a new treatment plan.\nYou will be given a medication called AC for 4 cycles, followed by T-DM1.\nYou will also need to have radiation treatment after completing AC.\nYou are currently taking oxyCODONE for pain.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will start the AC treatment and have an echocardiogram (a test to check your heart) before starting the treatment.\nYou will be referred to social work for support.\nYou will have an in-person visit this coming week to discuss the treatment recommendation.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

However, the cancer in other parts of your body, like your bones and lymph nodes, has not changed much.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will continue taking herceptin and letrozole. You will also keep taking a medication every 12 weeks because the cancer in your bones has not changed. You will not be on chemotherapy for now because you have had trouble with it before.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will have more imaging tests every 4 months to check on the cancer. This includes a CT scan of your chest and abdomen, and an MRI of your brain. You will also have a bone scan in 4 months. You are referred to a radiation oncologist for possible more radiation treatment to your brain. You will see your doctor again in 6 weeks for a follow-up visit.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a visit to discuss treatment options for your recently diagnosed breast cancer.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou were diagnosed with a type of breast cancer called ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS), which is cancer that started in the milk ducts. The cancer has not spread to other parts of your body. You had surgery to remove the cancer, and the area where the cancer was removed looks good with no signs of infection or fluid buildup. The main goal of your treatment is to reduce the risk of the cancer coming back.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou are being encouraged to think about taking a medication called tamoxifen for 5 years. This medication can help reduce the risk of the cancer coming back. You are also being encouraged to meet with a radiation oncologist to discuss whether radiation therapy might be helpful to reduce the risk of the cancer coming back in the same area.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are encouraged to meet with a radiation oncologist to discuss whether radiation therapy might be helpful for you. You will think about whether to take tamoxifen. We also discussed that you should consider lifestyle changes such as exercising, maintaining a healthy weight, and reducing alcohol intake.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

You are also receiving immunological vaccines once a week and taking pamidronate once a week.\nYou have stiffness in your back, but you are able to walk more now.\nYou have cancelled your CT scan for February 27 and plan to get scans in May.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou were given a prescription for Cymbalta, but you have not started taking it yet.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will continue with your current treatment plan, including the low-dose chemotherapy and immunological vaccines.\nYou will have a CT scan and a total spine MRI in May. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a second opinion regarding your newly diagnosed left breast cancer. The cancer is a mix of two types: one that started in the milk-producing glands and one that started in the milk ducts. You wanted to talk about your diagnosis, what might happen in the future, and the different ways to treat it.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a cancer in your left breast that is about 12 x 10 x 13 mm in size. It is close to the nipple. There is no sign that the cancer has spread to other parts of your body. The cancer is in an early stage, which is a good sign.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nNo changes were made to your current medications. If you choose to start medication treatment, the doctors think the a combination of chemotherapy and targeted therapy drugs regimen would be a good option.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to a breast surgery specialist for further treatment. The plan is to have surgery to remove the cancer. After the surgery, the doctors will decide on the next steps based on the results. Another option is to start medication treatment first to make the surgery easier.\nYou will have an ultrasound to check your breast. Biomarker testing will be done to help guide your treatment.\nYou will have another in-person visit later today to see your doctor.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a consultation regarding a metastatic recurrence of your breast cancer.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a history of left breast cancer with a 0.9cm grade II invasive ductal carcinoma (cancer that started in the milk ducts), and 1.8 cm of ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS). The cancer has now spread to your bones and lymph nodes.\nYour current treatment goals are to manage symptoms and improve your quality of life.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou have been started on Letrozole and Palbociclib. These medications are used to treat breast cancer.\nYou will also be starting on denosumab after a dental check-up. This medication helps prevent bone problems.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to a radiation oncologist for a consultation.\nYou will have an MRI of your spine and a CT scan of your chest, abdomen, and pelvis.\nYou will need monthly blood tests to check how you are responding to the medications.\nYou will have another visit in about one month to see how you are doing.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a second opinion regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a history of breast cancer that started in the milk ducts (invasive ductal carcinoma) and has spread to other parts of your body.\nThe cancer is now considered to be at a more advanced stage (Stage IV).\nYou have been on treatment, but you developed a lung condition called pneumonitis, which was thought to be due to a medication called abemaciclib. This medication was stopped since August 14, 2021.\nYour physical exam shows no new signs of cancer or other health issues.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYour treatment plan has been adjusted. A medication called letrozole was changed to anastrozole in July 2020 because of a skin rash.\nYou are currently taking steroids and anastrozole.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will have a PET scan to check how your cancer is responding to treatment.\nIf the PET scan shows that your cancer is stable, you will continue taking anastrozole.\nIf the PET scan shows that your cancer has progressed, your doctor may consider other treatments like faslodex, depending on your specific situation.\nFuture treatment options include hormone blockers like Afinitor or Xeloda, or you might be eligible for a clinical trial.\nYou are referred to for a follow-up visit after the PET scan results are in to decide the next steps.\nYou are currently a full code patient, meaning you want all possible life-saving measures if needed.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care."

Sentinel lymph node biopsy was positive and therefore the patient underwent a full axillary lymph node dissection on 04/12/2007 revealing April 07 lymph nodes positive.  The patient received 4 cycles of Adriamycin and Cytoxan followed by 4 cycles of Taxotere.  January 2008 the patient completed radiation to the right breast.  March 2008 the patient started adjuvant tamoxifen.  However she had a CT scan of the chest abdomen and pelvis that revealed an abnormality in the left breast.  08/23/2008 the patient underwent an MRI of bilateral breasts revealing a 12 mm mass in the lower inner quadrant of the left breast.  10/11/2008 the patient underwent left breast mammogram revealing a \n suspicious mass in the left breast.  10/30/2008 the patient underwent a fine-needle aspiration that revealed an invasive ductal carcinoma which was estrogen receptor positive, progesterone receptor negative, and HER-2/neu negative.  12/18/2008 the patient underwent a left breast lumpectomy and sentinel lymph node biopsy.  Pathology revealed a 1.5 cm grade 3 invasive ductal carcinoma which was estrogen receptor positive, progesterone receptor negative, and HER-2 negative by immunohistochemistry.  One out of the 3 sentinel lymph nodes was positive.  In December 2008 the patient had a completion axillary lymph node dissection and only 2 lymph nodes were seen both negative for cancer.  In January 2009, the patient initiated chemotherapy with Cytoxan, methotrexate, and 5-*****.  She also started Lupron at this time.  The patient underwent genetic testing and was negative for BRCA1 and 2.  08/27/2009 the patient underwent a CT scan of the chest abdomen and pelvis revealing enhancing tissue in the right anterior breast, a sclerotic focus in the left sacral ala, and a new sclerotic focus in the right anterior superior acetabulum. A bone scan on 09/04/2009 revealed no uptake to suggest osseous metastasis.  09/12/2009 the patient initiated postoperative radiation to the left breast, supraclavicular, and axillary regions receiving 1800 cGY to these regions.  09/20/2009, the patient underwent a core biopsy of a mass in the 12:00 position of the right breast which revealed a grade 1 invasive ductal carcinoma.  11/08/2009, the patient underwent a PET CT scan at ***** revealing skin thickening in the right breast, a 1.1 cm area of soft tissue enhancement in the right breast, no axillary or internal mammary adenopathy, and no clear evidence of distant metastatic disease.  12/14/2009 the patient underwent bilateral mastectomies, bilateral ***** flap reconstruction, and a left completion axillary \n dissection. Pathology from the left breast revealed no cancer, and 0/8 axillary lymph nodes contained metastatic cancer.  However in the right breast mastectomy specimen, there was was 2 cm of grade 1 invasive ductal cancer.  0 of 6 lymph nodes removed contained cancer on the right side.  The invasive cancer was again estrogen receptor positive, progesterone receptor negative, and HER-2/neu negative.  November 2009 the patient initiated letrozole.  October 2010 the patient began to experience some lightheadedness when standing.  She then developed more severe headaches.  Finally she noted a lump in the left side of her neck.  12/06/2010 the patient underwent a fine-needle aspiration of a left supraclavicular lymph node which revealed metastatic adenocarcinoma which was very weakly estrogen receptor positive with only 1-5% of the cells staining, progesterone receptor negative, and HER-2/neu negative.  12/11/2010 the patient underwent a PET CT scan which revealed a new right frontal dural based metastasis with SUV of 28.2 with surrounding vasogenic edema and mass effect.  The scan also revealed multiple hypermetabolic left cervical and supraclavicular lymph nodes consistent with metastatic disease.  There was hypermetabolism along the right parasternal chest wall consistent with recurrent disease possibly invading the sternum.  There was also bilateral subpectoral lymphadenopathy with maximum SUV of 11.4 on the left and 4.1 on the right. There was multifocal osseous metastatic disease identified within the C2, L2 vertebral bodies as well as in the sacrum.  12/11/2010 the patient underwent an MRI of the brain which revealed a 4 cm left inferior frontal extra-axial mass new since 11/08/2009.  Patient met with radiation oncology as \n well as neurosurgery to discuss treatment options.  12/30/2010 the patient underwent resection of the frontal brain metastasis. "Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a first visit regarding your newly diagnosed breast cancer.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which means the cancer started in the milk ducts. The cancer is in the left breast and has spread to some lymph nodes under your arm. No cancer has spread to other parts of your body.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will start a treatment plan with a medication called paclitaxel. This will be given weekly for 12 weeks. After that, you will have another type of treatment called AC for 4 cycles. This is to try to shrink the cancer before surgery.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will have a small device called a port placed in your body to make it easier to give you the medication. You will also have an echocardiogram to check your heart and an MRI of your breasts. Laboratory tests will be done as part of a study called ISPY. You will start your treatment within two weeks.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a first visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which means the cancer started in the milk ducts. It is in stage II or III, which means it has grown but has not spread to other parts of your body.\nThe cancer was removed with surgery, and no cancer was found in the removed tissue. This is a very good sign.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will be starting a medication to help prevent the cancer from coming back. This medication will be started after you have completed a process to preserve your eggs.\nYou are also taking a medication to help with nausea.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to have a CT scan and a bone scan to check for any signs of cancer in other parts of your body.\nYou will be having a test to learn more about your cancer and how it might respond to treatment.\nYou will have another visit with us in 3 weeks.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a second opinion regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called triple negative, which means the cancer does not respond to hormones or a protein called HER2. The cancer is currently in your breast and has not spread to other parts of your body. The goal of your treatment is to cure the cancer. The cancer is not responding as well as hoped, as there is still a tumor in your breast. During your physical exam, your left breast area where the surgery was done is healing well, but you have some swelling in your legs and tingling due to the chemotherapy.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nThe dose of your chemotherapy was reduced by 25% after the first cycle due to side effects.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are scheduled to receive radiation therapy in the near future. You are also recommended to have genetic counseling and testing.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a second opinion regarding your new diagnosis of high-grade invasive ductal carcinoma (a type of cancer that starts in the milk ducts) with spread to the lymph nodes in your armpit area.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou were diagnosed with a 2.1cm mass in your right breast and two lymph nodes that showed signs of cancer. Further tests confirmed that the cancer is a high-grade invasive ductal carcinoma.\nThe goal of your treatment is to cure the cancer.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will start neoadjuvant treatment (treatment given before surgery to shrink the cancer) with a combination of medications including docetaxel, carboplatin, trastuzumab, and pertuzumab.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to UCSF Breast Surgery for a consultation regarding surgery.\nBefore starting chemotherapy, you will have a TTE (a type of heart test).\nAfter surgery, you will be referred to Radiation Oncology.\nYou will also have genetic counseling and fertility referrals.\nWe will await the results of germline testing.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

STUDIES    PROCEDURE: PET SKULL BASE MID THIGH W ATTENUATION CT ***** *****, *****/*****/***** *****:***** PM    STUDY: F-18 FDG Whole Body PET/CT Study    CLINICAL INDICATION: 77-year-old woman with a history of right breast invasive lobular   carcinoma with right axillary nodal metastatic involvement, treated with letrozole (since   December 2019). Restaging evaluation/assessment of response to therapy.    COMPARISON: Prior PET/CT at ***** ***** ***** Imaging Center on 10/31/2019. A CT of the   chest, abdomen, and pelvis on 12/06/2019 and a whole body bone scan on 11/21/2019 are also   reviewed.    TECHNIQUE: At the time of this study, the patient's fasting blood glucose was 93 mg/dL and her   weight was 199 lbs. Approximately 70 minutes after the intravenous administration of 12.0 mCi   of 18-FDG, PET emission images and CT (dose-limited, without CT contrast) transmission images   were acquired from the top of the head to the mid thighs. The PET images were subsequently   corrected for tissue attenuation, fused with the CT images, and reconstructed for review in   orthogonal and 3D rotating (MIP) projections.  (CT dose information: CTDIvol = 9.9 mGy, DLP = 956.50 mGy-cm)    FINDINGS:  Head and Neck: There is relatively symmetric physiologic brain \n activity (though evaluation of   the brain on this study is limited). There is physiologic tonsillar, salivary gland, and oral   activity. There is mild atherosclerotic calcification of the carotid arteries, left slightly   greater than right. There is no discrete or intense thyroid hypermetabolism, nor intensely   hypermetabolic adenopathy within the neck.    Thorax: There is persistent subtle irregularity/nodularity of the bilateral breast tissues,   similar to prior study. In particular, there is an approximately 1.4 cm faintly hypermetabolic   density within the right lateral breast on CT images 119-***** with SUV 1.0 (previously similar   in size with SUV 0.8). There are other very faintly hypermetabolic nodular densities within   the both breasts, including an approximately 1 cm density within the right superolateral breast   (CT image 104, SUV 1.2) and an approximately 2 cm elongated density within the left lateral   breast (CT images 124-*****, SUV 1.3). These findings also appear relatively stable from prior   study. There are no definitive new or intensely hypermetabolic breast lesions on either side.    The numerous hypermetabolic right axillary and right subpectoral lymph nodes seen previously   has significantly improved with interval therapy. For example, there is a residual 2.1 x 1.0   cm right axillary node with an internal biopsy clip/marker on CT image 90 with SUV 1.9   (previously 2.3 x 1.5 cm with SUV 15.1), as well as a residual 1.2 x 1.0 cm right lateral   subpectoral node on CT images 83-84 with SUV 1.8 (previously 1.8 x 1.6 cm with SUV 9.6). There   are other scattered residual right axillary and right subpectoral nodes that have similarly   significantly declined in size from prior study, and now demonstrate little (if any)   appreciable metabolic activity. These findings are consistent with extensive locoregional   nodal metastatic disease that has substantially improved with \n interval therapy. Scattered   small, faintly hypermetabolic left axillary lymph nodes are most likely benign/reactive. There   is no significant hypermetabolic internal mammary, hilar, or mediastinal adenopathy.    There is a persistent Port-A-Cath within the left anterior upper thoracic wall (distal catheter   tip near *****/right atrial junction). Within the lungs, there are scattered areas of presumed   atelectasis or inflammation, particularly in the right thoracic paravertebral region (e.g. on   CT images 77-80 and 79-102), as well as presumed atelectasis or mild inflammation within the   lingula. Conversely, a prior hazy/ground-glass opacity within the posterior right lower lobe   has diminished or resolved. There are probable tiny, partially-calcified scars/granulomas   within the left apex laterally (CT image 76) and at the left posterior lung base (CT image   119), also seen previously. There is mild probable left apical scarring (CT image 71), as well   as a suspected tiny (3-4 mm) nodule within the right medial lung apex (CT image 74). There is   significant respiratory motion, limiting evaluation for other potential small/tiny lung   nodules. However, there are no discretely or intensely hypermetabolic lung nodules or masses.    There are no substantial pleural or pericardial effusions. There is suggestion of a small   hiatal hernia. The heart appears borderline/mildly enlarged, with mild coronary arterial   atherosclerotic calcification.    Abdomen and Pelvis: There is physiologic hepatic and splenic metabolic activity, with mildly   asymmetric elevation of the right hemidiaphragm. The gallbladder is not well evaluated on this   study, with suspected cholelithiasis (better seen previously). There is no significant   abnormal adrenal or pancreatic hypermetabolism. There is no evidence of an abdominal aortic   aneurysm.    There is physiologic GI activity, with relatively prominent, \n non-discrete proximal colonic   activity most likely reflecting normal variation. There is extensive stool within the rectum.    There is physiologic urinary radiotracer excretion, with moderate bilateral renal cortical   scarring. There is no definitive abnormal uterine hypermetabolism. The lower pelvis is again   suboptimally visualized on low-dose CT due to extensive streak artifacts from the patient's   right hip prosthesis. Small bilateral inguinal lymph nodes are likely benign/reactive, with no   intensely hypermetabolic subdiaphragmatic adenopathy.    Musculoskeletal: There is relatively prominent muscle tension/strain in the left subscapular   region and adjacent to both hips. There are other scattered areas of presumed subcutaneous   inflammation or edema, particularly within the patient's pannus bilaterally, lower back, and   right lateral upper thigh. There is also more prominent stranding/inflammation of the   subcutaneous fat in the left lateral abdominal and flank regions (e.g. on CT images 134-*****).   This is new from prior study, and may reflect interval trauma to this region.    There are chronic degenerative changes of the skeleton, with stable mild spinal scoliosis.   There is a persistent indwelling right hip prosthesis. There are persistent post-surgical   changes within the lower lumbar spine and left humeral head, as well as probable post-surgical   changes of the right posterior ilium (e.g. a bone graft donor site). There are probable   old/healed rib injuries (e.g. within the right posterior ribcage), as well as probable new   traumatic injury/fracture of the left 12th rib (SUV 3.7). Prior activity near the left   posterior acetabulum/ischium has resolved. A prior discrete focus of hypermetabolism within   the right inferior L5 vertebra (near the right lumbosacral junction) has also resolved, as has   prior hypermetabolism within the left posterior third rib and right posterior \n ninth rib. These   prior findings were equivocal, with limited, treated/resolved (by metabolism) skeletal   metastatic disease not entirely excluded. However, there is currently felt to be no definitive   metabolic evidence of active skeletal metastatic disease.    For reference/comparison purposes, blood pool activity within the descending thoracic aorta (at   the level of the carina) demonstrates an approximate SUV of 2.0 currently and 2.2 previously. There is also more prominent stranding/inflammation of the   subcutaneous fat in the left lateral abdominal and flank regions (e.g. on CT images 134-*****).    This is new from prior study, and may reflect interval trauma to this region.    There are chronic degenerative changes of the skeleton, with stable mild spinal scoliosis.    There is a persistent indwelling right hip prosthesis.  There are persistent post-surgical   changes within the lower lumbar spine and left humeral head, as well as probable post-surgical   changes of the right posterior ilium (e.g. a bone graft donor site).  There are probable   old/healed rib injuries (e.g. within the right posterior ribcage), as well as probable new   traumatic injury/fracture of the left 12th rib (SUV 3.7).  Prior activity near the left   posterior acetabulum/ischium has resolved.  A prior discrete focus of hypermetabolism within   the right inferior L5 vertebra (near the right lumbosacral junction) has also resolved, as has   prior hypermetabolism within the left posterior third rib and right posterior ninth rib.  These   prior findings were equivocal, with limited, treated/resolved (by metabolism) skeletal   metastatic disease not entirely excluded.  \n However, there is currently felt to be no definitive   metabolic evidence of active skeletal metastatic disease.    For reference/comparison purposes, blood pool activity within the descending thoracic aorta (at   the level of the carina) demonstrates an approximate SUV of 2.0 currently and 2.2 previously.      IMPRESSION:    1.  Relatively stable faintly hypermetabolic nodular densities within both breasts, as detailed   above.  The apparent limited FDG avidity of this patient's primary breast cancer limits its   evaluation by this imaging technique.    2.  Prior extensive locoregional nodal metastatic disease in the right axillary and right   subpectoral regions has substantially improved with interval therapy.  A few residual   borderline-enlarged, faintly hypermetabolic lymph nodes in these areas suggest potential   limited residual nodal metastatic disease.    3.  New probable inflammatory changes within the left lateral abdominal wall/flank soft   tissues, with a new probable injury/fracture of the left 12th rib.  There are probable   superimposed old/healed rib injuries.  A few other equivocal skeletal finding seen previously   have resolved (metabolically), with limited treated/resolved skeletal metastatic disease not   entirely excluded.    4.  Probable combination of scars/granulomas and atelectatic changes within the lungs, as   detailed above.    5.  Other likely-benign findings, as detailed above. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a new patient evaluation regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nYou have a history of breast cancer that started in the milk ducts (ductal carcinoma). The cancer was in the left breast and had spread to 23 lymph nodes. The cancer was also found in your brain, lungs, and lymph nodes. You had surgery to remove one of the cancer areas in your brain and received radiation to both areas. You are currently taking a medicine called Xeloda. No new imaging results are mentioned in this visit.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou are currently taking Xeloda. If the cancer tests positive for HER2, you may need a different treatment.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will have tests done to check the cancer in your brain and the remaining cancer in your body. You will be checked again in 3 months if you are doing well. If the cancer gets worse while you are on Xeloda, you may be considered for clinical trials or other treatments. You are set to follow up with your doctor as needed or if the cancer gets worse.\nWe understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did you come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a follow-up visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What's new or changed since your last visit?**\nThe cancer is responding to treatment. MRI shows that the tumor in your left breast has gotten smaller.\nThe cancer is still in your left armpit area, but it has gotten smaller.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will start a new treatment called AC chemotherapy.\nYou will also continue taking a medication called prilosec to help with heartburn.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to a radiation oncologist to discuss radiation treatment for your breast and armpit area.\nAfter radiation treatment, you will start a medication called capecitabine.\nYou are strongly recommended to take a medication to prevent the cancer from coming back after all treatments.\nYou will have another in-person visit after the radiation treatment is done.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"
